\t\t\t\t\t<a href="#p-search">search</a>\n\t\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t\t\t<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For the album by Pips, Chips &amp; Videoclips, see <a href="/wiki/Fred_Astaire_(album)" title="Fred Astaire (album)">Fred Astaire (album)</a>.</div>\n<table class="infobox <i>You\'ll Never Get Rich</i> (1941)</div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">Born</th>\n<td><span class="nickname">Frederick Austerlitz</span><br />\n<span style="display:none">(<span scope="row">Children</th>\n<td>2</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<th scope="row">Relatives</th>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Adele_Astaire" title="Adele Astaire">Adele Astaire</a> <small>(sister)</small></td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<p><b>Fred Astaire</b> (born <b>Frederick Austerlitz</b>;<sup id="cite_ref-Billman_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Billman-1">[1]</a></sup> May 10, 1899&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 June 22, 1987) was an American <a href="/wiki/Dance" title="Dance">dancer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singer" class="mw-redirect" title="Singer">singer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Actor" title="Actor">actor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Choreographer" class="mw-redirect" title="Choreographer">choreographer</a> and television presenter. He is widely regarded as one of the most influential dancers in the history of film and television musicals.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></p>\n<p>His stage and subsequent film and television careers spanned a total of 76 years, during which he starred in more than 10 Broadway and London <a href="/wiki/Musicals" class="mw-redirect" title="Musicals">musicals</a>, made 31 <a href="/wiki/Musical_film" title="Musical film">musical films</a>, 4 television specials, and issued numerous recordings. As a dancer, he is best remembered for his sense of rhythm, his perfectionism, and as the dancing partner and on-screen romantic interest of <a href="/wiki/Ginger_Rogers" title="Ginger Rogers">Ginger Rogers</a>, with whom he co-starred in a series of ten Hollywood musicals. Astaire was named by the <a href="/wiki/American_Film_Institute" title="American Film Institute">American Film Institute</a> as the fifth greatest male star of Classic Hollywood cinema in <a href="/wiki/AFI%27s_100_Years...100_Stars" title="AFI\'s 100 Years...100 Stars">100 Years... 100 Stars</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Gene_Kelly" title="Gene Kelly">Gene Kelly</a>, another star in filmed dance, said that "the history of dance on film begins with Astaire." Later, he asserted that Astaire was "the only one of today\'s dancers who will be remembered."<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Beyond film and television, many dancers and choreographers, including <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Nureyev" title="Rudolf Nureyev">Rudolf Nureyev</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sammy_Davis_Jr." title="Sammy Davis Jr.">Sammy Davis Jr.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michael_Jackson" title="Michael Jackson">Michael Jackson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Hines" title="Gregory Hines">Gregory Hines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Baryshnikov" title="Mikhail Baryshnikov">Mikhail Baryshnikov</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Balanchine" title="George Balanchine">George Balanchine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jerome_Robbins" title="Jerome Robbins">Jerome Robbins</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Madhuri_Dixit" title="Madhuri Dixit">Madhuri Dixit</a>, also acknowledged his influence.</p>\n<p></p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc">\n<div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr" xml:lang="en">\n<h2>Contents</h2>\n</div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Life_and_career"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Life and class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#1948\xe2\x80\x931957:_Productive_years_with_MGM_and_second_retirement"><span class="tocnumber">1.5</span> <span class="toctext">1948\xe2\x80\x931957: Productive years with MGM and second retirement</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#1957\xe2\x80\x931981:_Branching_out_into_televised_dance_and_straight_acting"><span class="tocnumber">1.6</span> <span class="toctext">1957\xe2\x80\x931981: Branching out into televised dance and straight acting</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-8"><a work</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Films,_musical"><span class="tocnumber">7.1</span> <span class="toctext">Films, musical</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 href="#Television"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">Television</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#References"><span links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<p></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_and_career">Life and career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fred_Astaire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Life and career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="1899.E2.80.931917:_Early_life_and_career"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1899\xe2\x80\x931917:_Early_life_and_career">1899\xe2\x80\x931917: Early life and career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fred_Astaire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 1899\xe2\x80\x931917: Early life and career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vcvg16.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Vcvg16.jpg/220px-Vcvg16.jpg" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Vcvg16.jpg/330px-Vcvg16.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Vcvg16.jpg/440px-Vcvg16.jpg 2x" data-file-width="520" data-file-height="392" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vcvg16.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nFred and his sister Adele in 1906</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Astaire was born in <a href="/wiki/Omaha,_Nebraska" title="Omaha, Nebraska">Omaha, Nebraska</a>, the son of Johanna "Ann" (n\xc3\xa9e Geilus) and Frederic "Fritz" <a href="/wiki/Austerlitz_(family)" title="Austerlitz (family)">Austerlitz</a> (born September 8, 1868 as Friedrich Emanuel Austerlitz).<sup id="cite_ref-Billman_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Billman-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fred_Astaire_(1899-1987)_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fred_Astaire_(1899-1987)-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AdeleAusterlitzBiog_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AdeleAusterlitzBiog-7">[7]</a></sup> Astaire\'s mother was born in the United States, to <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> German emigrants from <a href="/wiki/East_Prussia" title="East Prussia">East Prussia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alsace" title="Alsace">Alsace</a>. Astaire\'s father was born in <a href="/wiki/Linz" title="Linz">Linz</a>, Austria, to <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jewish</a> parents who had converted to <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Roman Catholicism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Billman_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Billman-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Austerlitz_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Austerlitz-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bookref09_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bookref09-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>\n<p>After arriving in New York City at age 25 on October 26, 1893, and being inspected at <a href="/wiki/Ellis_Island" title="Ellis Island">Ellis Island</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Astaire\'s father, hoping to find work in his brewing trade, moved to Omaha, Nebraska, and landed a job with the <a href="/wiki/Storz_Brewing_Company" title="Storz Brewing Company">Storz Brewing Company</a>. Astaire\'s mother dreamed of escaping Omaha by virtue of her children\'s talents, after Astaire\'s sister, <a href="/wiki/Adele_Astaire" title="Adele Astaire">Adele Astaire</a>, early on revealed herself to be an instinctive dancer and singer. She planned a "brother and sister act," which was common in <a href="/wiki/Vaudeville" title="Vaudeville">vaudeville</a> at the time. Although Astaire refused dance lessons at first, he easily mimicked his older sister\'s steps and took up piano, <a href="/wiki/Accordion" title="Accordion">accordion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Clarinet" title="Clarinet">clarinet</a>.</p>\n<p>When their father suddenly lost his job, the family moved to New York City in 1905 to launch the show business career of the children, who began training at the Alviene Master School of the Theatre and Academy of Cultural Arts.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-12">[12]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Despite Adele and Fred\'s teasing rivalry, they quickly acknowledged their individual strengths, his durability and her greater talent. Fred and Adele\'s mother suggested they change their name to "Astaire," as she felt "Austerlitz" sounded reminiscent of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Austerlitz" title="Battle of Austerlitz">the name of a battle</a>. Family legend attributes the name to an uncle surnamed "L\'Astaire."<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> They were taught dance, speaking, and singing in preparation for developing an act. Their first act was called <i>Juvenile Artists Presenting an Electric Musical Toe-Dancing Novelty</i>. Fred wore a top hat and tails in the first half and a lobster outfit in the second. In an interview, Astaire\'s daughter, Ava Astaire McKenzie, observed that they often put Fred in a top hat to make him look taller.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The goofy act debuted in <a href="/wiki/Keyport,_New_Jersey" title="Keyport, New Jersey">Keyport, New Jersey</a>, in a "tryout theater." The local paper wrote, "the Astaires are the greatest child act in vaudeville."<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>\n<p>As a result of their father\'s salesmanship, Fred and Adele rapidly landed a major contract and played the famed <a href="/wiki/Orpheum_Circuit" title="Orpheum Circuit">Orpheum Circuit</a> in the Midwest, Western and some Southern cities in the United States. Soon Adele grew to at least three inches taller than Fred and the pair began to look incongruous. The family decided to take a two-year break from show business to let time take its course and to avoid trouble from the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Society_for_the_Prevention_of_Cruelty_to_Children" title="New York Society for the Prevention of Cruelty to Children">Gerry Society</a> and the child labor laws of the time. In 1912, Fred became an <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_Church_(United_States)" title="Episcopal Church (United States)">Episcopalian</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Astaire_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Astaire-16">[16]</a></sup> The career of the Astaire siblings resumed with mixed fortunes, though with increasing skill and polish, as they began to incorporate <a href="/wiki/Tap_dance" title="Tap dance">tap dancing</a> into their routines. Astaire\'s dancing was inspired by <a href="/wiki/Bill_Robinson" title="Bill Robinson">Bill "Bojangles" Robinson</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_W._Bubbles" title="John W. Bubbles">John "Bubbles" Sublett</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> From vaudeville dancer <a href="/w/index.php?title=Aurelio_Coccia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Aurelio Coccia (page does not exist)">Aurelio Coccia</a>, they learned the tango, waltz, and other ballroom dances popularized by <a href="/wiki/Vernon_and_Irene_Castle" title="Vernon and Irene Castle">Vernon and Irene Castle</a>. Some sources<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> state that the Astaire siblings appeared in a 1915 film titled <i>Fanchon, the Cricket</i>, starring <a href="/wiki/Mary_Pickford" title="Mary Pickford">Mary Pickford</a>, but the Astaires have consistently denied this.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>\n<p>By age 14, Fred had taken on the musical responsibilities for their act.<sup id="cite_ref-nyt_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-12">[12]</a></sup> He first met <a href="/wiki/George_Gershwin" title="George Gershwin">George Gershwin</a>, who was working as a <a href="/wiki/Song_plugger" title="Song plugger">song plugger</a> for <a href="/wiki/Jerome_H._Remick" title="Jerome H. Remick">Jerome H. Remick</a>\'s music publishing company, in 1916.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Fred had already been hunting for new music and dance ideas. Their chance meeting was to deeply affect the careers of both artists. Astaire was always on the lookout for new steps on the circuit and was starting to demonstrate his ceaseless quest for novelty and perfection. The Astaires broke into Broadway in 1917 with <i>Over the Top</i>, a patriotic revue, and performed for U.S. and Allied troops at this time as well.</p>\n<h3><span id="1917.E2.80.931933:_Stage_career_on_Broadway_and_in_London"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1917\xe2\x80\x931933:_Stage_career_on_Broadway_and_in_London">1917\xe2\x80\x931933: Stage career on Broadway and in London</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fred_Astaire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1917\xe2\x80\x931933: Stage career on Broadway and in London">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AdeleFred1921.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/AdeleFred1921.jpg/220px-AdeleFred1921.jpg" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/AdeleFred1921.jpg/330px-AdeleFred1921.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/AdeleFred1921.jpg/440px-AdeleFred1921.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="498" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AdeleFred1921.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nFred and Adele Astaire in 1921</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The Astaires followed up with several more shows, and of their work in "<a href="/wiki/The_Passing_Show_of_1918" title="The Passing Show of 1918">The Passing Show of 1918</a>," <a href="/wiki/Heywood_Broun" title="Heywood Broun">Heywood Broun</a> wrote: "In an evening in which there was an abundance of good dancing, Fred Astaire stood out&#160;... He and his partner, Adele Astaire, made the show pause early in the evening with a beautiful loose-limbed dance."<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>\n<p>By this time, Astaire\'s dancing skill was beginning to outshine his sister\'s, though she still set the tone of their act and her sparkle and humor drew much of the attention, owing in part to Fred\'s careful preparation and strong supporting choreography.</p>\n<p>During the 1920s, Fred and Adele appeared on Broadway and on the <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> stage in shows such as <a href="/wiki/Jerome_Kern" title="Jerome Kern">Jerome Kern</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bunch_and_Judy" title="The Bunch and Judy">The Bunch and Judy</a></i> (1922), George and <a href="/wiki/Ira_Gershwin" title="Ira Gershwin">Ira Gershwin</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Lady,_Be_Good_(musical)" title="Lady, Be Good (musical)">Lady, Be Good</a></i> (1924), and <i><a href="/wiki/Funny_Face_(musical)" title="Funny Face (musical)">Funny Face</a></i> (1927) and later in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Band_Wagon_(musical)" title="The Band Wagon (musical)">The Band Wagon</a></i> (1931), winning popular acclaim with the theater crowd on both sides of the Atlantic. By then, Astaire\'s tap dancing was recognized as among the best, as <a href="/wiki/Robert_Benchley" title="Robert Benchley">Robert Benchley</a> wrote in 1930, "I don\'t think that I will plunge the nation into war by stating that Fred is the greatest tap-dancer in the world."<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>5</span></sup></p>\n<p>After the close of <i>Funny Face</i>, the Astaires went to <a href="/wiki/Hollywood" title="Hollywood">Hollywood</a> for a screen test (now lost) at <a href="/wiki/Paramount_Pictures" title="Paramount Pictures">Paramount Pictures</a>, but Paramount deemed them unsuitable for films.</p>\n<p>They split in 1932 when Adele married her first husband, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Charles_Arthur_Francis_Cavendish" title="Lord Charles Arthur Francis Cavendish">Lord Charles Cavendish</a>, second son of the <a href="/wiki/Victor_Cavendish,_9th_Duke_of_Devonshire" title="Victor Cavendish, 9th Duke of Devonshire">9th Duke of Devonshire</a>. Fred went on to achieve success on his own on Broadway and in London with <i><a href="/wiki/Gay_Divorce" title="Gay Divorce">Gay Divorce</a></i> (later made into the film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Gay_Divorcee" title="The Gay Divorcee">The Gay Divorcee</a></i>), while considering offers from Hollywood. The end of the partnership was traumatic for Astaire but stimulated him to expand his range.</p>\n<p>Free of the brother-sister constraints of the former pairing and working with new partner <a href="/wiki/Claire_Luce" title="Claire Luce">Claire Luce</a>, Fred created a romantic partnered dance to <a href="/wiki/Cole_Porter" title="Cole Porter">Cole Porter</a>\'s "<a href="/wiki/Night_and_Day_(song)" title="Night and Day (song)">Night and Day</a>," which had been written for <i>Gay Divorce</i>. Luce stated that she had to encourage him to take a more romantic approach: "Come on, Fred, I\'m not your sister, you know."<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>6</span></sup> The success of the stage play was credited to this number and, when recreated in <i>The Gay Divorcee</i> (1934), the film version of the play, it ushered in a new era in filmed dance.<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>23,26,61</span></sup> Recently, film footage taken by <a href="/wiki/Fred_Stone" title="Fred Stone">Fred Stone</a> of Astaire performing in <i>Gay Divorce</i> with Luce\'s successor, Dorothy Stone, in New York in 1933 was uncovered by dancer and historian Betsy Baytos and now represents the earliest known performance footage of Astaire.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span id="1933.E2.80.931939:_Astaire_and_Rogers_at_RKO"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1933\xe2\x80\x931939:_Astaire_and_Rogers_at_RKO">1933\xe2\x80\x931939: Astaire and Rogers at RKO</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fred_Astaire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: 1933\xe2\x80\x931939: Astaire and Rogers at RKO">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Top-Hat-Rogers-Astaire.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Top-Hat-Rogers-Astaire.jpg/220px-Top-Hat-Rogers-Astaire.jpg" width="220" height="277" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Top-Hat-Rogers-Astaire.jpg/330px-Top-Hat-Rogers-Astaire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Top-Hat-Rogers-Astaire.jpg/440px-Top-Hat-Rogers-Astaire.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2350" data-file-height="2960" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Top-Hat-Rogers-Astaire.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/Ginger_Rogers" title="Ginger Rogers">Ginger Rogers</a> and Fred Astaire in <i><a href="/wiki/Top_Hat" title="Top Hat">Top Hat</a></i> (1935)</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>According to Hollywood folklore, a screen test report on Astaire for <a href="/wiki/RKO_Pictures" title="RKO Pictures">RKO Radio Pictures</a>, now lost along with the test, is reported to have read: "Can\'t sing. Can\'t act. Balding. Can dance a little." The producer of the Astaire-Rogers pictures, <a href="/wiki/Pandro_S._Berman" title="Pandro S. Berman">Pandro S. Berman</a>, claimed he had never heard the story in the 1930s and that it only emerged years afterwards.<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>7</span></sup> Astaire later clarified, insisting that the report had actually read: "Can\'t act. Slightly bald. <i>Also dances.</i>"<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> In any case, the test was clearly disappointing, and <a href="/wiki/David_O._Selznick" title="David O. Selznick">David&#160;O. Selznick</a>, who had signed Astaire to RKO and commissioned the test, stated in a memo, "I am uncertain about the man, but I feel, in spite of his enormous ears and bad chin line, that his charm is so tremendous that it comes through even on this wretched test."<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>7</span></sup></p>\n<p>However, this did not affect RKO\'s plans for Astaire, first lending him for a few days to <a href="/wiki/Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer" title="Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer">MGM</a> in 1933 for his significant Hollywood debut, where he appeared as himself dancing with <a href="/wiki/Joan_Crawford" title="Joan Crawford">Joan Crawford</a> in the successful musical film <i><a href="/wiki/Dancing_Lady" title="Dancing Lady">Dancing Lady</a></i>. On his return to RKO, he got fifth billing after fourth billed Ginger Rogers in the 1933 <a href="/wiki/Dolores_del_R%C3%ADo" title="Dolores del R\xc3\xado">Dolores del R\xc3\xado</a> vehicle <i><a href="/wiki/Flying_Down_to_Rio" title="Flying Down to Rio">Flying Down to Rio</a></i>. In a review, <i><a href="/wiki/Variety_(magazine)" title="Variety (magazine)">Variety</a></i> magazine attributed its massive success to Astaire\'s presence:</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>The main point of <i>Flying Down to Rio</i> is the screen promise of Fred Astaire&#160;... He\'s assuredly a bet after this one, for he\'s distinctly likable on the screen, the mike is kind to his voice and as a dancer he remains in a class by himself. The latter observation will be no news to the profession, which has long admitted that Astaire starts dancing where the others stop hoofing.<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>7</span></sup></p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Having already been linked to his sister Adele on stage, Astaire was initially very reluctant to become part of another dance team. He wrote his agent, "I don\'t mind making another picture with her, but as for this \'team\' idea, it\'s \'out!\' I\'ve just managed to live down one partnership and I don\'t want to be bothered with any more."<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>8</span></sup> However, he was persuaded by the obvious public appeal of the Astaire-Rogers pairing. The partnership, and the choreography of Astaire and <a href="/wiki/Hermes_Pan" title="Hermes Pan">Hermes Pan</a>, helped make dancing an important element of the Hollywood <a href="/wiki/Musical_film" title="Musical film">film musical</a>.</p>\n<p>Astaire and Rogers made nine films together at RKO, including <i>The Gay Divorcee</i> (1934), <i><a href="/wiki/Roberta_(1935_film)" title="Roberta (1935 film)">Roberta</a></i> (1935, in which Astaire also demonstrates his oft-overlooked piano skills with a spirited solo on "I Won\'t Dance"), <i><a href="/wiki/Top_Hat" title="Top Hat">Top Hat</a></i> (1935), <i><a href="/wiki/Follow_the_Fleet" title="Follow the Fleet">Follow the Fleet</a></i> (1936), <i><a href="/wiki/Swing_Time_(film)" title="Swing Time (film)">Swing Time</a></i> (1936), <i><a href="/wiki/Shall_We_Dance_(1937_film)" title="Shall We Dance (1937 film)">Shall We Dance</a></i> (1937), and <i><a href="/wiki/Carefree_(film)" title="Carefree (film)">Carefree</a></i> (1938). Six out of the nine Astaire-Rogers musicals became the biggest moneymakers for RKO; all of the films brought a certain prestige and artistry that all studios coveted at the time. Their partnership elevated them both to stardom; as <a href="/wiki/Katharine_Hepburn" title="Katharine Hepburn">Katharine Hepburn</a> reportedly said, "He gives her class and she gives him sex appeal."<sup id="cite_ref-Croce_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Croce-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>134</span></sup> Astaire received a percentage of the films\' profits, something extremely rare in actors\' contracts at that time.</p>\n<p>Astaire was also given complete autonomy over how the dances would be presented, allowing him to revolutionize dance on film.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> He is credited with two important innovations in early film musicals.<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>23,26</span></sup> First, he insisted that a closely tracking dolly camera film a dance routine in as few shots as possible, typically with just four to eight cuts, while holding the dancers in full view at all times. This gave the illusion of an almost stationary camera filming an entire dance in a single shot. Astaire famously quipped: "Either the camera will dance, or I will."<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>420</span></sup> Astaire maintained this policy from <i>The Gay Divorcee</i> in 1934 onwards until his last film musical, <i><a href="/wiki/Finian%27s_Rainbow_(film)" title="Finian\'s Rainbow (film)">Finian\'s Rainbow</a></i>, made in 1968, when he was overruled by director <a href="/wiki/Francis_Ford_Coppola" title="Francis Ford Coppola">Francis Ford Coppola</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Astaire\'s style of dance sequences, which allowed the viewer to follow the dancers and choreography in their entirety, clearly contrasted with the <a href="/wiki/Busby_Berkeley" title="Busby Berkeley">Busby Berkeley</a> musicals, which were known for dance sequences filled with extravagant aerial shots and dozens of cuts for quick takes and zooms on certain areas of the body, such as a chorus row of arms or legs.</p>\n<p>Astaire\'s second innovation involved the context of the dance; he was adamant that all song and dance routines be seamlessly integrated into the plotlines of the film. Instead of using dance as spectacle as Busby Berkeley did, Astaire used it to move the plot along. Typically, an Astaire picture would include at least three standard dances: a solo performance by Astaire\xe2\x80\x94which he termed his "sock solo," a partnered comedy dance routine, and a partnered romantic dance routine.</p>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fredginger.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/97/Fredginger.jpg/220px-Fredginger.jpg" width="220" height="238" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/9/97/Fredginger.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="293" data-file-height="317" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fredginger.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nAn RKO publicity still of Astaire and Rogers dancing to "<a href="/wiki/Smoke_Gets_in_Your_Eyes" title="Smoke Gets in Your Eyes">Smoke Gets in Your Eyes</a>" in <i><a href="/wiki/Roberta_(1935_film)" title="Roberta (1935 film)">Roberta</a></i> (1935)</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Dance commentators <a href="/wiki/Arlene_Croce" title="Arlene Croce">Arlene Croce</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Croce_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Croce-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>6</span></sup> Hannah Hyam<sup id="cite_ref-Hyam_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hyam-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>146,147</span></sup> and <a href="/wiki/John_Mueller" title="John Mueller">John Mueller</a><sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>8,9</span></sup> consider Rogers to have been Astaire\'s greatest dance partner, a view shared<sup id="cite_ref-GilesP33_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GilesP33-31">[31]</a></sup> by Hermes Pan and <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Donen" title="Stanley Donen">Stanley Donen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-GilesP33_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GilesP33-31">[31]</a></sup> Film critic <a href="/wiki/Pauline_Kael" title="Pauline Kael">Pauline Kael</a> adopts a more neutral stance,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> while <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine film critic <a href="/wiki/Richard_Schickel" title="Richard Schickel">Richard Schickel</a> writes "The nostalgia surrounding Rogers-Astaire tends to bleach out other partners."<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Mueller sums up Rogers\'s abilities as follows:</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>Rogers was outstanding among Astaire\'s partners not because she was superior to others as a dancer, but because, as a skilled, intuitive actress, she was cagey enough to realize that acting did not stop when dancing began&#160;... the reason so many women have fantasized about dancing with Fred Astaire is that Ginger Rogers conveyed the impression that dancing with him is the most thrilling experience imaginable.<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup></p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>According to Astaire, "Ginger had never danced with a partner before <i>Flying Down to Rio</i>. She faked it an awful lot. She couldn\'t tap and she couldn\'t do this and that&#160;... but Ginger had style and talent and improved as she went along. She got so that after a while everyone else who danced with me looked wrong."<sup id="cite_ref-Satchell1_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Satchell1-34">[34]</a></sup> In his book <i>Ginger: Salute to a Star</i> author Dick Richards quotes Astaire saying to Raymond Rohauer, curator of the New York Gallery of Modern Art, "Ginger was brilliantly effective. She made everything work for her. Actually she made things very fine for both of us and she deserves most of the credit for our success." When asked who his favorite dancing partner was by British TV interviewer <a href="/wiki/Michael_Parkinson" title="Michael Parkinson">Michael Parkinson</a> on <i><a href="/wiki/Parkinson_(TV_series)" title="Parkinson (TV series)">Parkinson</a></i> in 1976, Astaire said "Excuse me, I must say Ginger was certainly the one. You know, the most effective partner I had. Everyone knows. That was a whole other thing what we did...I just want to pay a tribute to Ginger because we did so many pictures together and believe me it was a value to have that girl...she had it! She was just great!"</p>\n<p>For her part, Rogers described Astaire\'s uncompromising standards extending to the whole production: "Sometimes he\'ll think of a new line of dialogue or a new angle for the story&#160;... they never know what time of night he\'ll call up and start ranting enthusiastically about a fresh idea&#160;... No loafing on the job on an Astaire picture, and no cutting corners."<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>16</span></sup></p>\n<p>Astaire was still unwilling to have his career tied exclusively to any partnership, however. He negotiated with RKO to strike out on his own with <i><a href="/wiki/A_Damsel_in_Distress" title="A Damsel in Distress">A Damsel in Distress</a></i> in 1937 with an inexperienced, non-dancing <a href="/wiki/Joan_Fontaine" title="Joan Fontaine">Joan Fontaine</a>, unsuccessfully as it turned out. He returned to make two more films with Rogers, <i>Carefree</i> (1938) and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Story_of_Vernon_and_Irene_Castle" title="The Story of Vernon and Irene Castle">The Story of Vernon and Irene Castle</a></i> (1939). While both films earned respectable gross incomes, they both lost money because of increased production costs,<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>410</span></sup> and Astaire left RKO, after being labeled "<a href="/wiki/Box_Office_Poison_(magazine_article)" title="Box Office Poison (magazine article)">box office poison</a>" by the <i>Independent Film Journal</i>. Astaire was reunited with Rogers in 1949 at MGM for their final outing, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Barkleys_of_Broadway" title="The Barkleys of Broadway">The Barkleys of Broadway</a></i>, the only one of their films together to be shot in <a href="/wiki/Technicolor" title="Technicolor">Technicolor</a>.</p>\n<h3><span id="1940.E2.80.931947:_Drifting_to_an_early_retirement"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1940\xe2\x80\x931947:_Drifting_to_an_early_retirement">1940\xe2\x80\x931947: Drifting to an early retirement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fred_Astaire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1940\xe2\x80\x931947: Drifting to an early retirement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation">\n<tr>\n<td class="mbox-image">\n<div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div>\n</td>\n<td class="mbox-text">\n<div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Fred_Astaire&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small><i>(June 2017)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BroadwayMelody1940.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/51/BroadwayMelody1940.JPG/220px-BroadwayMelody1940.JPG" width="220" height="314" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/5/51/BroadwayMelody1940.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="264" data-file-height="377" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BroadwayMelody1940.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nWith <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Powell" title="Eleanor Powell">Eleanor Powell</a> in <i>Broadway Melody of 1940</i></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>In 1939, Astaire left RKO to freelance and pursue new film opportunities, with mixed though generally successful outcomes. Throughout this period, Astaire continued to value the input of choreographic collaborators and, unlike the 1930s when he worked almost exclusively with Hermes Pan, he tapped the talents of other choreographers in an effort to continually innovate. His first post-Ginger dance partner was the redoubtable <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Powell" title="Eleanor Powell">Eleanor Powell</a>\xe2\x80\x94considered the finest female tap-dancer of her generation\xe2\x80\x94in <i><a href="/wiki/Broadway_Melody_of_1940" title="Broadway Melody of 1940">Broadway Melody of 1940</a></i>, in which they performed a celebrated extended dance routine to Cole Porter\'s "<a href="/wiki/Begin_the_Beguine" title="Begin the Beguine">Begin the Beguine</a>." In his autobiography <i>Steps in Time</i>, Astaire remarked, "She \'put \'em down like a man,\' no ricky-ticky-sissy stuff with Ellie. She really knocked out a tap dance in a class by herself."</p>\n<p>He played alongside <a href="/wiki/Bing_Crosby" title="Bing Crosby">Bing Crosby</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Holiday_Inn_(film)" title="Holiday Inn (film)">Holiday Inn</a></i> (1942) and later <i><a href="/wiki/Blue_Skies_(1946_film)" title="Blue Skies (1946 film)">Blue Skies</a></i> (1946) but, in spite of the enormous financial success of both, was reportedly dissatisfied with roles where he lost the girl to Crosby. The former film is particularly remembered for his virtuoso solo dance to "Let\'s Say it with Firecrackers" while the latter film featured an innovative song and dance routine to a song indelibly associated with him: "<a href="/wiki/Puttin%27_On_the_Ritz" title="Puttin\' On the Ritz">Puttin\' On the Ritz</a>." Other partners during this period included <a href="/wiki/Paulette_Goddard" title="Paulette Goddard">Paulette Goddard</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Second_Chorus" title="Second Chorus">Second Chorus</a></i> (1940), in which he dance-conducted the <a href="/wiki/Artie_Shaw" title="Artie Shaw">Artie Shaw</a> orchestra.</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Astaire-Hayworth-dancing.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Astaire-Hayworth-dancing.JPG/220px-Astaire-Hayworth-dancing.JPG" width="220" height="291" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Astaire-Hayworth-dancing.JPG/330px-Astaire-Hayworth-dancing.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Astaire-Hayworth-dancing.JPG/440px-Astaire-Hayworth-dancing.JPG 2x" data-file-width="694" data-file-height="918" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Astaire-Hayworth-dancing.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nWith <a href="/wiki/Rita_Hayworth" title="Rita Hayworth">Rita Hayworth</a> in <i>You Were Never Lovelier</i> (1942)</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>He made two pictures with <a href="/wiki/Rita_Hayworth" title="Rita Hayworth">Rita Hayworth</a>, the daughter of his former vaudeville dance idols, the Cansinos. The first, <i><a href="/wiki/You%27ll_Never_Get_Rich" title="You\'ll Never Get Rich">You\'ll Never Get Rich</a></i> (1941), catapulted Hayworth to stardom and provided Astaire his third on-screen opportunity to integrate Latin American dance idioms into his style (the first being with Ginger Rogers in "The Carioca" number from <i>Flying Down to Rio</i> (1933) and the second, again with Rogers, was the "Dengozo" dance from <i>The Story of Vernon and Irene Castle</i> (1939)), taking advantage of Hayworth\'s professional Latin dance pedigree. His second film with Hayworth, <i><a href="/wiki/You_Were_Never_Lovelier" title="You Were Never Lovelier">You Were Never Lovelier</a></i> (1942), was equally successful and featured a duet to Kern\'s "<a href="/wiki/I%27m_Old_Fashioned" title="I\'m Old Fashioned">I\'m Old Fashioned</a>," which became the centerpiece of <a href="/wiki/Jerome_Robbins" title="Jerome Robbins">Jerome Robbins</a>\'s 1983 <a href="/wiki/New_York_City_Ballet" title="New York City Ballet">New York City Ballet</a> tribute to Astaire. He next appeared opposite the seventeen-year-old <a href="/wiki/Joan_Leslie" title="Joan Leslie">Joan Leslie</a> in the wartime drama <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sky%27s_the_Limit_(1943_film)" title="The Sky\'s the Limit (1943 film)">The Sky\'s the Limit</a></i> (1943), in which he introduced <a href="/wiki/Harold_Arlen" title="Harold Arlen">Arlen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Mercer" title="Johnny Mercer">Mercer</a>\'s "<a href="/wiki/One_for_My_Baby_(and_One_More_for_the_Road)" title="One for My Baby (and One More for the Road)">One for My Baby</a>" while dancing on a bar counter in a dark and troubled routine. This film, which was choreographed by Astaire alone and achieved modest box office success, represented an important departure for Astaire from his usual charming happy-go-lucky screen persona, and confused contemporary critics.</p>\n<p>His next partner, <a href="/wiki/Lucille_Bremer" title="Lucille Bremer">Lucille Bremer</a>, was featured in two lavish vehicles, both directed by <a href="/wiki/Vincente_Minnelli" title="Vincente Minnelli">Vincente Minnelli</a>: the fantasy <i><a href="/wiki/Yolanda_and_the_Thief" title="Yolanda and the Thief">Yolanda and the Thief</a></i>, which featured an avant-garde surrealistic ballet, and the musical revue <i><a href="/wiki/Ziegfeld_Follies_(film)" title="Ziegfeld Follies (film)">Ziegfeld Follies</a></i> (1946), which featured a memorable teaming of Astaire with Gene Kelly to "The Babbit and the Bromide," a Gershwin song Astaire had introduced with his sister Adele back in 1927. While <i>Follies</i> was a hit, <i>Yolanda</i> bombed at the box office, and Astaire, ever insecure and believing his career was beginning to falter, surprised his audiences by announcing his retirement during the production of <i>Blue Skies</i> (1946), nominating "Puttin\' on the Ritz" as his farewell dance.</p>\n<p>After announcing his retirement in 1946, Astaire concentrated on his horse-racing interests and in 1947 founded the <a href="/wiki/Fred_Astaire_Dance_Studios" title="Fred Astaire Dance Studios">Fred Astaire Dance Studios</a>, which he subsequently sold in 1966.</p>\n<h3><span id="1948.E2.80.931957:_Productive_years_with_MGM_and_second_retirement"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1948\xe2\x80\x931957:_Productive_years_with_MGM_and_second_retirement">1948\xe2\x80\x931957: Productive years with MGM and second retirement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fred_Astaire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 1948\xe2\x80\x931957: Productive years with MGM and second retirement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Astaire,_Fred_-_Daddy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Astaire%2C_Fred_-_Daddy.jpg/220px-Astaire%2C_Fred_-_Daddy.jpg" width="220" height="277" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Astaire%2C_Fred_-_Daddy.jpg/330px-Astaire%2C_Fred_-_Daddy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Astaire%2C_Fred_-_Daddy.jpg/440px-Astaire%2C_Fred_-_Daddy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1590" data-file-height="2000" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Astaire,_Fred_-_Daddy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nIn <i>Daddy Long Legs</i> (1955)</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Retirement didn\'t last long. Astaire returned to the big screen to replace the injured Kelly in <i><a href="/wiki/Easter_Parade_(film)" title="Easter Parade (film)">Easter Parade</a></i> (1948) opposite <a href="/wiki/Judy_Garland" title="Judy Garland">Judy Garland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ann_Miller" title="Ann Miller">Ann Miller</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Lawford" title="Peter Lawford">Peter Lawford</a> and for a final reunion with Rogers (replacing Judy Garland) in <i>The Barkleys of Broadway</i> (1949). Both of these films revived Astaire\'s popularity and in 1950 he starred in two musicals - one for M-G-M - <i><a href="/wiki/Three_Little_Words_(film)" title="Three Little Words (film)">Three Little Words</a></i> with <a href="/wiki/Vera-Ellen" title="Vera-Ellen">Vera-Ellen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Red_Skelton" title="Red Skelton">Red Skelton</a> and one on loan-out to Paramount - <i><a href="/wiki/Let%27s_Dance_(1950_film)" title="Let\'s Dance (1950 film)">Let\'s Dance</a></i> with <a href="/wiki/Betty_Hutton" title="Betty Hutton">Betty Hutton</a>. While <i>Three Little Words</i> did quite well at the box office, <i>Let\'s Dance</i> was a financial disappointment. <i><a href="/wiki/Royal_Wedding" title="Royal Wedding">Royal Wedding</a></i> (1951) with <a href="/wiki/Jane_Powell" title="Jane Powell">Jane Powell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Lawford" title="Peter Lawford">Peter Lawford</a> proved to be very successful, but <i><a href="/wiki/The_Belle_of_New_York_(1952_film)" title="The Belle of New York (1952 film)">The Belle of New York</a></i> (1952) with Vera-Ellen was a critical and box-office disaster. <i><a href="/wiki/The_Band_Wagon" title="The Band Wagon">The Band Wagon</a></i> (1953), which is considered to be one of the finest musicals ever made, received rave reviews from critics and drew huge crowds. But because of its excessive cost, it failed to make a profit on its first release. Soon after, Astaire, along with all the other remaining stars at M-G-M, was let go from his contract because of the advent of television and the downsizing of film production. In 1954, Astaire was about to start work on a new musical, <i><a href="/wiki/Daddy_Long_Legs_(1955_film)" title="Daddy Long Legs (1955 film)">Daddy Long Legs</a></i> (1955) with <a href="/wiki/Leslie_Caron" title="Leslie Caron">Leslie Caron</a> at <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" title="20th Century Fox">20th Century Fox</a>, when his wife Phyllis became ill and suddenly died of lung cancer. Astaire was so bereaved that he wanted to shut down the picture and offered to pay the production costs out of his own pocket. However, <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Mercer" title="Johnny Mercer">Johnny Mercer</a> (the film\'s composer) and Fox studio executives convinced him that work would be the best thing for him at that time. When <i>Daddy Long Legs</i> was released in 1955, it did only moderately well at the box office. His next film for Paramount, <i><a href="/wiki/Funny_Face" title="Funny Face">Funny Face</a></i> (1957), teamed him with <a href="/wiki/Audrey_Hepburn" title="Audrey Hepburn">Audrey Hepburn</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kay_Thompson" title="Kay Thompson">Kay Thompson</a> and despite the sumptuousness of the production and the strong reviews from critics, it failed to make back its cost. Similarly, Astaire\'s next project - his final musical at M-G-M, <i><a href="/wiki/Silk_Stockings_(film)" title="Silk Stockings (film)">Silk Stockings</a></i> (1957), in which he co-starred with <a href="/wiki/Cyd_Charisse" title="Cyd Charisse">Cyd Charisse</a>, also lost money at the box office. As a result, Astaire withdrew from motion pictures for two years.</p>\n<p>During 1952, Astaire recorded <i><a href="/wiki/The_Astaire_Story" title="The Astaire Story">The Astaire Story</a></i>, a four-volume album with a quintet led by <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Peterson" title="Oscar Peterson">Oscar Peterson</a>. The album, produced by <a href="/wiki/Norman_Granz" title="Norman Granz">Norman Granz</a>, provided a musical overview of Astaire\'s career. <i>The Astaire Story</i> later won the <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Hall_of_Fame" title="Grammy Hall of Fame">Grammy Hall of Fame Award</a> in 1999, a special Grammy award to honor recordings that are at least twenty-five years old and that have "qualitative or historical significance."<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>\n<p>His legacy at this point was 30 musical films in 25 years. Afterwards, Astaire announced that he was retiring from dancing in film to concentrate on dramatic acting, scoring rave reviews for the nuclear war drama <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Beach_(1959_film)" title="On the Beach (1959 film)">On the Beach</a></i> (1959).</p>\n<h3><span id="1957.E2.80.931981:_Branching_out_into_televised_dance_and_straight_acting"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1957\xe2\x80\x931981:_Branching_out_into_televised_dance_and_straight_acting">1957\xe2\x80\x931981: Branching out into televised dance and straight acting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fred_Astaire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 1957\xe2\x80\x931981: Branching out into televised dance and straight acting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Astaire did not retire from dancing completely. He made a series of four highly rated <a href="/wiki/Emmy_Award" title="Emmy Award">Emmy Award</a>-winning musical specials for television in 1958, 1959, 1960, and 1968, each featuring <a href="/wiki/Barrie_Chase" title="Barrie Chase">Barrie Chase</a>, with whom Astaire enjoyed a renewed period of dance creativity. The first of these programs, 1958\'s <i><a href="/wiki/An_Evening_with_Fred_Astaire" title="An Evening with Fred Astaire">An Evening with Fred Astaire</a></i>, won nine Emmy Awards, including "Best Single Performance by an Actor" and "Most Outstanding Single Program of the Year." It was also noteworthy for being the first major broadcast to be prerecorded on color videotape and has recently been restored. The restoration won a technical Emmy in 1988 for Ed Reitan, Don Kent, and Dan Einstein, who restored the original videotape, transferring its contents to a modern format and filling in gaps where the tape had deteriorated with kinescope footage. Astaire won the Emmy for Best Single Performance by an Actor, but the choice had a controversial backlash because many believed that his dancing in the special was not the type of "acting" for which the award was designed. At one point Astaire offered to return the award, but the Television Academy refused to consider it.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Astaire played Julian Osborne, a non-dancing character, in the 1959 movie <i>On the Beach</i> and was nominated for a Golden Globe Best Supporting Actor award for his performance, losing to <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Boyd" title="Stephen Boyd">Stephen Boyd</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Ben-Hur_(1959_film)" title="Ben-Hur (1959 film)">Ben-Hur</a></i>. Astaire appeared in non-dancing roles in three other films and several television series from 1957 to 1969.</p>\n<p>Astaire\'s last major musical film was <i><a href="/wiki/Finian%27s_Rainbow_(film)" title="Finian\'s Rainbow (film)">Finian\'s Rainbow</a></i> (1968), directed by <a href="/wiki/Francis_Ford_Coppola" title="Francis Ford Coppola">Francis Ford Coppola</a>. Astaire shed his white tie and tails to play an Irish rogue who believes that if he buries a crock of gold in the shadows of <a href="/wiki/Fort_Knox" title="Fort Knox">Fort Knox</a> the gold will multiply. Astaire\'s dance partner was <a href="/wiki/Petula_Clark" title="Petula Clark">Petula Clark</a>, who played his character\'s skeptical daughter. He described himself as nervous about singing with her, while she said she was worried about dancing with him. The film was a modest success both at the box office and among critics.</p>\n<p>Astaire continued to act in the 1970s, appearing on television as the father of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Wagner" title="Robert Wagner">Robert Wagner</a>\'s character, Alexander Mundy, in <i><a href="/wiki/It_Takes_a_Thief_(1968_TV_series)" title="It Takes a Thief (1968 TV series)">It Takes a Thief</a></i> and in such films as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Towering_Inferno" title="The Towering Inferno">The Towering Inferno</a></i> (1974), in which he danced with <a href="/wiki/Jennifer_Jones" title="Jennifer Jones">Jennifer Jones</a> and for which he received his only <a href="/wiki/Academy_Awards" title="Academy Awards">Academy Award</a> nomination, in the category of <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Supporting_Actor" title="Academy Award for Best Supporting Actor">Best Supporting Actor</a>. He voiced the mailman narrator in the 1970s animated television specials <i><a href="/wiki/Santa_Claus_Is_Comin%27_to_Town_(film)" title="Santa Claus Is Comin\' to Town (film)">Santa Claus Is Comin\' to Town</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Easter_Bunny_Is_Comin%27_to_Town" title="The Easter Bunny Is Comin\' to Town">The Easter Bunny Is Comin\' to Town</a></i>. Astaire also appeared in the first two <i><a href="/wiki/That%27s_Entertainment!" title="That\'s Entertainment!">That\'s Entertainment!</a></i> documentaries, in the mid 1970s. In the second compilation, aged seventy-six, he performed brief dance linking sequences with Kelly, his last dance performances in a musical film. In the summer of 1975, he made three albums in London, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Attitude_Dancing_(album)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Attitude Dancing (album) (page does not exist)">Attitude Dancing</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/They_Can%27t_Take_These_Away_from_Me" title="They Can\'t Take These Away from Me">They Can\'t Take These Away from Me</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/A_Couple_of_Song_and_Dance_Men" title="A Couple of Song and Dance Men">A Couple of Song and Dance Men</a></i>, the last an album of duets with Bing Crosby. In 1976, Astaire played a supporting role, as a dog owner, in the cult movie <i><a href="/wiki/The_Amazing_Dobermans" title="The Amazing Dobermans">The Amazing Dobermans</a></i>, co-starring <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Eden" title="Barbara Eden">Barbara Eden</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_Franciscus" title="James Franciscus">James Franciscus</a>. Fred Astaire played Dr. Seamus Scully in the <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French</a> <a href="/wiki/Film" title="Film">film</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Purple_Taxi" title="The Purple Taxi">The Purple Taxi</a></i> (1977).</p>\n<p>In 1978, he co-starred with <a href="/wiki/Helen_Hayes" title="Helen Hayes">Helen Hayes</a> in a well received television film, <i>A Family Upside Down,</i> in which they played an elderly couple coping with failing health. Astaire won an Emmy Award for his performance. He made a well publicized guest appearance on the <a href="/wiki/Science_fiction" title="Science fiction">science-fiction</a> television series <i><a href="/wiki/Battlestar_Galactica_(1978_TV_series)" title="Battlestar Galactica (1978 TV series)">Battlestar Galactica</a></i> in 1979, as Chameleon, the possible father of <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_Starbuck" title="Lieutenant Starbuck">Starbuck</a>, in "The Man with Nine Lives," a role written for him by <a href="/wiki/Donald_P._Bellisario" title="Donald P. Bellisario">Donald P. Bellisario</a>. Astaire asked his agent to obtain a role for him on <i>Galactica</i> because of his grandchildren\'s interest in the series. This episode marked the final time that he danced on screen. He acted nine different roles in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Man_in_the_Santa_Claus_Suit" title="The Man in the Santa Claus Suit">The Man in the Santa Claus Suit</a></i> in 1979. His final film role was the 1981 adaptation of <a href="/wiki/Peter_Straub" title="Peter Straub">Peter Straub</a>\'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Ghost_Story_(1981_film)" title="Ghost Story (1981 film)">Ghost Story</a></i>. This horror film was also the last for two of his most prominent castmates, <a href="/wiki/Melvyn_Douglas" title="Melvyn Douglas">Melvyn Douglas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Fairbanks_Jr." title="Douglas Fairbanks Jr.">Douglas Fairbanks Jr.</a></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Working_methods_and_influence_on_filmed_dance">Working methods and influence on filmed dance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fred_Astaire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Working methods and influence on filmed dance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Fred_Astaire%27s_solo_and_partnered_dances" title="Fred Astaire\'s solo and partnered dances">Fred Astaire\'s solo and partnered dances</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Astaire_-_You%27re_All_the_World_to_Me.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Astaire_-_You%27re_All_the_World_to_Me.jpg/220px-Astaire_-_You%27re_All_the_World_to_Me.jpg" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a0/Astaire_-_You%27re_All_the_World_to_Me.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="310" data-file-height="240" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Astaire_-_You%27re_All_the_World_to_Me.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nAstaire dancing on the walls and ceiling for "You\'re All the World to Me"<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> from <i><a href="/wiki/Royal_Wedding" title="Royal Wedding">Royal Wedding</a></i> (1951)</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Astaire was a virtuoso dancer, able to convey light-hearted venturesomeness or deep emotion when called for. His technical control and sense of rhythm were astonishing. Long after the photography for the solo dance number "I Want to Be a Dancin\' Man" was completed for the 1952 feature <i>The Belle of New York</i>, it was decided that Astaire\'s humble costume and the threadbare stage set were inadequate and the entire sequence was reshot. The 1994 documentary <i><a href="/wiki/That%27s_Entertainment!_III" title="That\'s Entertainment! III">That\'s Entertainment! III</a></i> shows the two performances side-by-side in split screen. Frame for frame, the two performances are absolutely identical, down to the subtlest gesture.</p>\n<p>Astaire\'s execution of a dance routine was prized for its elegance, grace, originality, and precision. He drew from a variety of influences, including tap and other black rhythms, classical dance, and the elevated style of <a href="/wiki/Vernon_and_Irene_Castle" title="Vernon and Irene Castle">Vernon and Irene Castle</a> to create a uniquely recognizable dance style which greatly influenced the <a href="/wiki/American_Smooth" class="mw-redirect" title="American Smooth">American Smooth</a> style of <a href="/wiki/Ballroom_dance" title="Ballroom dance">ballroom dance</a> and set standards against which subsequent film dance musicals would be judged. He termed his eclectic approach his "outlaw style," an unpredictable and instinctive blending of personal artistry. His dances are economical yet endlessly nuanced. As <a href="/wiki/Jerome_Robbins" title="Jerome Robbins">Jerome Robbins</a> stated, "Astaire\'s dancing looks so simple, so disarming, so easy, yet the understructure, the way he sets the steps on, over or against the music, is so surprising and inventive."<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>18</span></sup> Astaire further observed:</p>\n<blockquote class="templatequote">\n<p>Working out the steps is a very complicated process\xe2\x80\x94something like writing music. You have to think of some step that flows into the next one, and the whole dance must have an integrated pattern. If the dance is right, there shouldn\'t be a single superfluous movement. It should build to a climax and stop!<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>15</span></sup></p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>With very few exceptions, Astaire created his routines in collaboration with other choreographers, primarily <a href="/wiki/Hermes_Pan" title="Hermes Pan">Hermes Pan</a>. They would often start with a blank slate:</p>\n<blockquote class="templatequote">\n<p>For maybe a couple of days we wouldn\'t get anywhere\xe2\x80\x94just stand in front of the mirror and fool around... Then suddenly I\'d get an idea or one of them would get an idea... So then we\'d get started... You might get practically the whole idea of the routine done that day, but then you\'d work on it, edit it, scramble it, and so forth. It might take sometimes as long as two, three weeks to get something going.<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>15</span></sup></p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Frequently, a dance sequence was built around two or three principal ideas, sometimes inspired by his own steps or by the music itself, suggesting a particular mood or action.<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>20</span></sup> Many of his dances were built around a "gimmick," such as dancing on the walls in "Royal Wedding" or dancing with his shadows in <i><a href="/wiki/Swing_Time_(film)" title="Swing Time (film)">Swing Time</a></i>, that he or his collaborator had thought up earlier and saved for the right situation. They would spend weeks creating all the dance sequences in a secluded rehearsal space before filming would begin, working with a rehearsal pianist (often the composer <a href="/wiki/Hal_Borne" title="Hal Borne">Hal Borne</a>) who in turn would communicate modifications to the musical orchestrators.</p>\n<p>His perfectionism was legendary; however, his relentless insistence on rehearsals and retakes was a burden to some. When time approached for the shooting of a number, Astaire would rehearse for another two weeks and record the singing and music. With all the preparation completed, the actual shooting would go quickly, conserving costs. Astaire agonized during the entire process, frequently asking colleagues for acceptance for his work. As <a href="/wiki/Vincente_Minnelli" title="Vincente Minnelli">Vincente Minnelli</a> stated, "He lacks confidence to the most enormous degree of all the people in the world. He will not even go to see his rushes... He always thinks he is no good."<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>16</span></sup> As Astaire himself observed, "I\'ve never yet got anything 100% right. Still it\'s never as bad as I think it is."<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>16</span></sup></p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Michael_Kidd" title="Michael Kidd">Michael Kidd</a>, who choreographed the 1953 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Band_Wagon" title="The Band Wagon">The Band Wagon</a></i>, found that his own concern about the emotional motivation behind the dance was not shared by Astaire. Kidd later recounted: "Technique was important to him. He\'d say, \'Let\'s do the steps. Let\'s add the looks later.\' "<sup id="cite_ref-Kisselgoff_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kisselgoff-38">[38]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Although he viewed himself as an entertainer first and foremost, his consummate artistry won him the admiration of such twentieth century dance legends as Gene Kelly, George Balanchine, the <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Brothers" title="Nicholas Brothers">Nicholas Brothers</a>, Mikhail Baryshnikov, <a href="/wiki/Margot_Fonteyn" title="Margot Fonteyn">Margot Fonteyn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bob_Fosse" title="Bob Fosse">Bob Fosse</a>, Gregory Hines, Rudolf Nureyev, Michael Jackson, and <a href="/wiki/Bill_Robinson" title="Bill Robinson">Bill Robinson</a>. Balanchine compared him to <a href="/wiki/Johann_Sebastian_Bach" title="Johann Sebastian Bach">Bach</a>, describing him as "<a href="https://en.wikiquote.org/wiki/Fred_Astaire#Classical_dancers_and_choreographers_on_Astaire" class="extiw" title="q:Fred Astaire">the most interesting, the most inventive, the most elegant dancer of our times</a>," while for Baryshnikov he was "<a href="https://en.wikiquote.org/wiki/Fred_Astaire#Classical_dancers_and_choreographers_on_Astaire" class="extiw" title="q:Fred Astaire">a genius... a classical dancer like I never saw in my life</a>."</p>\n<p>Fred Astaire performed dance in four ways. He performed romantic ballroom duets, tap duets, solos, and solos with props. He also usually danced with a partner, for example Ginger Rogers. A very well known duet with Rogers that he did was \xe2\x80\x9cCheek to Cheek\xe2\x80\x9d which is a beautiful dance that looks almost like they are gliding across the dance floor together. He didn\xe2\x80\x99t just perform romantic duets with Rogers. He also did many tap duet numbers with her, like the dance \xe2\x80\x9cLet Yourself Go\xe2\x80\x9d in <i>Follow the Fleet</i>. Astaire\xe2\x80\x99s tap solos, however, seemed to be the most popular. People were amazed at the things he could dance with while performing. From coat trees to drum sets, Astaire could do it all.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_popular_song">Influence on popular song</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fred_Astaire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Influence on popular song">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_songs_introduced_by_Fred_Astaire" title="List of songs introduced by Fred Astaire">List of songs introduced by Fred Astaire</a></div>\n<p>Extremely modest about his singing abilities (he frequently claimed that he could not sing,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> but the critics rated him as among the finest), Astaire introduced some of the most celebrated songs from the <a href="/wiki/Great_American_Songbook" title="Great American Songbook">Great American Songbook</a>, in particular, Cole Porter\'s: "Night and Day" in <i>Gay Divorce</i> (1932) and "<a href="/wiki/So_Near_and_yet_So_Far" title="So Near and yet So Far">So Near and yet So Far</a>" in <i>You\'ll Never Get Rich</i> (1941), <a href="/wiki/Irving_Berlin" title="Irving Berlin">Irving Berlin</a>\'s "<a href="/wiki/Isn%27t_This_a_Lovely_Day%3F" title="Isn\'t This a Lovely Day?">Isn\'t This a Lovely Day?</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Cheek_to_Cheek" title="Cheek to Cheek">Cheek to Cheek</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Top_Hat,_White_Tie_and_Tails" title="Top Hat, White Tie and Tails">Top Hat, White Tie and Tails</a>" in <i>Top Hat</i> (1935), "<a href="/wiki/Let%27s_Face_the_Music_and_Dance" title="Let\'s Face the Music and Dance">Let\'s Face the Music and Dance</a>" in <i>Follow the Fleet</i> (1936) and "<a href="/wiki/Change_Partners" title="Change Partners">Change Partners</a>" in <i>Carefree</i> (1938). He first presented <a href="/wiki/Jerome_Kern" title="Jerome Kern">Jerome Kern</a>\'s "<a href="/wiki/The_Way_You_Look_Tonight" title="The Way You Look Tonight">The Way You Look Tonight</a>" in <i><a href="/wiki/Swing_Time_(film)" title="Swing Time (film)">Swing Time</a></i> (1936); <a href="/wiki/George_Gershwin" title="George Gershwin">the Gershwins\'</a> "<a href="/wiki/They_Can%27t_Take_That_Away_from_Me" title="They Can\'t Take That Away from Me">They Can\'t Take That Away from Me</a>" in <i><a href="/wiki/Shall_We_Dance_(1937_film)" title="Shall We Dance (1937 film)">Shall We Dance</a></i> (1937), "<a href="/wiki/A_Foggy_Day" title="A Foggy Day">A Foggy Day</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Nice_Work_If_You_Can_Get_It_(song)" title="Nice Work If You Can Get It (song)">Nice Work if You Can Get it</a>" in <i><a href="/wiki/A_Damsel_in_Distress" title="A Damsel in Distress">A Damsel in Distress</a></i> (1937); <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Mercer" title="Johnny Mercer">Johnny Mercer</a>\'s "<a href="/wiki/One_for_My_Baby_(and_One_More_for_the_Road)" title="One for My Baby (and One More for the Road)">One for My Baby</a>" from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Sky%27s_the_Limit_(1943_film)" title="The Sky\'s the Limit (1943 film)">The Sky\'s the Limit</a></i> (1943) and "<a href="/wiki/Something%27s_Gotta_Give_(Johnny_Mercer_song)" title="Something\'s Gotta Give (Johnny Mercer song)">Something\'s Gotta Give</a>" from <i><a href="/wiki/Daddy_Long_Legs_(1955_film)" title="Daddy Long Legs (1955 film)">Daddy Long Legs</a></i> (1955); and <a href="/wiki/Harry_Warren" title="Harry Warren">Harry Warren</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Freed" title="Arthur Freed">Arthur Freed</a>\'s "This Heart of Mine" from <i><a href="/wiki/Ziegfeld_Follies_(film)" title="Ziegfeld Follies (film)">Ziegfeld Follies</a></i> (1946).</p>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Astaire_singing_in_Second_Chorus.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Astaire_singing_in_Second_Chorus.jpg/220px-Astaire_singing_in_Second_Chorus.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Astaire_singing_in_Second_Chorus.jpg/330px-Astaire_singing_in_Second_Chorus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Astaire_singing_in_Second_Chorus.jpg/440px-Astaire_singing_in_Second_Chorus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="962" data-file-height="722" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Astaire_singing_in_Second_Chorus.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nAstaire singing in <i><a href="/wiki/Second_Chorus" title="Second Chorus">Second Chorus</a></i> (1940)</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Astaire also co-introduced a number of song classics via song duets with his partners. For example, with his sister Adele, he co-introduced the Gershwins\' "I\'ll Build a <a href="/wiki/Stairway_to_Paradise" title="Stairway to Paradise">Stairway to Paradise</a>" from <i>Stop Flirting</i> (1923), "<a href="/wiki/Fascinating_Rhythm" title="Fascinating Rhythm">Fascinating Rhythm</a>" in <i>Lady, Be Good</i> (1924), "<a href="/wiki/Funny_Face_(1927_song)" title="Funny Face (1927 song)">Funny Face</a>" in <i>Funny Face</i> (1927); and, in duets with Ginger Rogers, he presented Irving Berlin\'s "<a href="/wiki/I%27m_Putting_All_My_Eggs_in_One_Basket" title="I\'m Putting All My Eggs in One Basket">I\'m Putting All My Eggs in One Basket</a>" in <i>Follow the Fleet</i> (1936), Jerome Kern\'s "<a href="/wiki/Pick_Yourself_Up" title="Pick Yourself Up">Pick Yourself Up</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/A_Fine_Romance_(song)" title="A Fine Romance (song)">A Fine Romance</a>" in <i><a href="/wiki/Swing_Time_(film)" title="Swing Time (film)">Swing Time</a></i> (1936), along with The Gershwins\' "<a href="/wiki/Let%27s_Call_the_Whole_Thing_Off" title="Let\'s Call the Whole Thing Off">Let\'s Call the Whole Thing Off</a>" from <i><a href="/wiki/Shall_We_Dance_(1937_film)" title="Shall We Dance (1937 film)">Shall We Dance</a></i> (1937). With <a href="/wiki/Judy_Garland" title="Judy Garland">Judy Garland</a>, he sang Irving Berlin\'s "A Couple of Swells" from <i>Easter Parade</i> (1948); and, with <a href="/wiki/Jack_Buchanan" title="Jack Buchanan">Jack Buchanan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Levant" title="Oscar Levant">Oscar Levant</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nanette_Fabray" title="Nanette Fabray">Nanette Fabray</a> he delivered <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schwartz" title="Arthur Schwartz">Arthur Schwartz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Howard_Dietz" title="Howard Dietz">Howard Dietz</a>\'s "That\'s Entertainment" from <i>The Band Wagon</i> (1953).</p>\n<p>Although he possessed a light voice, he was admired for his lyricism, diction, and phrasing<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>\xe2\x80\x94the grace and elegance so prized in his dancing seemed to be reflected in his singing, a capacity for synthesis which led <a href="/wiki/Burton_Lane" title="Burton Lane">Burton Lane</a> to describe him as "the world\'s greatest musical performer."<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>21</span></sup> Irving Berlin considered Astaire the equal of any male interpreter of his songs\xe2\x80\x94"as good as <a href="/wiki/Al_Jolson" title="Al Jolson">Jolson</a>, Crosby or <a href="/wiki/Frank_Sinatra" title="Frank Sinatra">Sinatra</a>, not necessarily because of his voice, but for his conception of projecting a song."<sup id="cite_ref-en.wikiquote.org_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-en.wikiquote.org-42">[42]</a></sup> Jerome Kern considered him the supreme male interpreter of his songs<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>21</span></sup> and Cole Porter and Johnny Mercer also admired his unique treatment of their work. And while George Gershwin was somewhat critical of Astaire\'s singing abilities, he wrote many of his most memorable songs for him.<sup id="cite_ref-Mueller_24-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mueller-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>123,128</span></sup> In his heyday, Astaire was referenced<sup id="cite_ref-en.wikiquote.org_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-en.wikiquote.org-42">[42]</a></sup> in lyrics of songwriters Cole Porter, <a href="/wiki/Lorenz_Hart" title="Lorenz Hart">Lorenz Hart</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eric_Maschwitz" title="Eric Maschwitz">Eric Maschwitz</a> and continues to inspire modern songwriters.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Astaire was a songwriter of note himself, with "I\'m Building Up to an Awful Letdown" (written with lyricist <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Mercer" title="Johnny Mercer">Johnny Mercer</a>) reaching number four in the Hit Parade of 1936.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> He recorded his own "It\'s Just Like Taking Candy from a Baby" with <a href="/wiki/Benny_Goodman" title="Benny Goodman">Benny Goodman</a> in 1940 and nurtured a lifelong ambition to be a successful popular song composer.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span id="Awards.2C_honors_and_tributes"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards,_honors_and_tributes">Awards, honors and tributes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Fred_Astaire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Awards, honors and tributes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FredAstairesFeet.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/FredAstairesFeet.jpg/220px-FredAstairesFeet.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/FredAstairesFeet.jpg/330px-FredAstairesFeet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/FredAstairesFeet.jpg/440px-FredAstairesFeet.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FredAstairesFeet.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nAstaire\'s hand and foot prints at Grauman\'s Chinese Theater</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lismore_Astaire_plaque.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Lismore_Astaire_plaque.jpg/220px-Lismore_Astaire_plaque.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Lismore_Astaire_plaque.jpg/330px-Lismore_Astaire_plaque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Lismore_Astaire_plaque.jpg/440px-Lismore_Astaire_plaque.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lismore_Astaire_plaque.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nPlaque honoring Astaire in Lismore, Waterford, Ireland</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<ul>\n<li>1938: Invited to place his hand and foot prints in cement at <a href="/wiki/TCL_Chinese_Theatre" class="mw-redirect" title="TCL Chinese Theatre">Grauman\'s Chinese Theatre</a>, Hollywood<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1950: Ginger Rogers presented an honorary <a href="/wiki/Academy_Awards" title="Academy Awards">Academy Award</a> to Astaire "for his unique artistry and his contributions to the technique of musical pictures"</li>\n<li>1950: <a href="/wiki/Golden_Globe_Award" title="Golden Globe Award">Golden Globe</a> for "Best Motion Picture \xe2\x80\x93 Musical or Comedy" for <i><a href="/wiki/Three_Little_Words_(film)" title="Three Little Words (film)">Three Little Words</a></i></li>\n<li>1958: <a href="/wiki/Emmy_Award" title="Emmy Award">Emmy Award</a> for "Best Single Performance by an Actor" for <i><a href="/wiki/An_Evening_with_Fred_Astaire" title="An Evening with Fred Astaire">An Evening with Fred Astaire</a></i></li>\n<li>1959: <a href="/wiki/Dance_Magazine" title="Dance Magazine">Dance Magazine</a> award</li>\n<li>1960: Nominated for Emmy Award for "Program Achievement" for <i>Another Evening with Fred Astaire</i></li>\n<li>1960: <a href="/wiki/Golden_Globe_Cecil_B._DeMille_Award" title="Golden Globe Cecil B. DeMille Award">Golden Globe Cecil B.
